People v. Thomas


Docket No. 79493


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


178 Ill. 2d 215; 687 N.E.2d 892; 1997 Ill. LEXIS 426; 227 Ill. Dec. 410



September 18, 1997, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied June 26, 1998, Reported at: 1998 U.S. LEXIS 4337.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court of Lake County, the Hon. Charles F. Scott, Judge, presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review from a judgment of the Circuit Court of Lake County (Illinois), which convicted him of murder and imposed a sentence of death after finding that defendant was eligible for the death penalty based upon the statutory aggravating factor that the murder occurred during the course of a felony, 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/9-1(b)(6).


OVERVIEW: The court held that defendant was proven guilty beyond a reasonable doubt. The fact that the occurrence witnesses gave conflicting descriptions of the shooter was not fatal to the state's ability to prove that defendant committed the murder. In reviewing the evidence in favor of the state, a rational jury could have believed defendant's own statement that he shot the victim. In addition to defendant's statements, other evidence also linked defendant to the crime. The court also held that defendant's failure to raise the issue regarding the testimony of a co-conspirator's wife below resulted in waiver on appeal. The court noted that when the trial court agreed to admit the handwritten, typed, and videotaped statements as substantive evidence, defense counsel voiced only a general objection. The use of the co-conspirator's wife's statement as substantive evidence at defendant's trial did not deny defendant his constitutional right to confront witnesses because she testified at trial. The court concluded that the trial court did not violate the Eighth Amendment in considering defendant's antisocial personality disorder and antisocial behavior as aggravating evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment of conviction and sentencing.

People v. Hickey

SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


178 Ill. 2d 256; 687 N.E.2d 910; 1997 Ill. LEXIS 431; 227 Ill. Dec. 428



September 18, 1997, Opinion Filed

He challenged various comments made by the prosecutor and the verdict that he had committed the felony of home invasion. The court affirmed the convictions and the sentences. 85 of 1262 DOCUMENTS


Atkins v. Deere & Co.


Docket Nos. 82220, 82221 cons.


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


177 Ill. 2d 222; 685 N.E.2d 342; 1997 Ill. LEXIS 403; 226 Ill. Dec. 239



September 11, 1997, Opinion Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] No. 82220-Affirmed and remanded; No. 82221-Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted leave to appeal the judgments of two Illinois circuit courts to determine whether the repeal of the Illinois Structural Work Act (Act), 740 Ill. Comp. Stat. 150/0.01 et seq. (1994), operated as a bar to all causes of actions which had accrued prior to the effective date of the repeal. Plaintiff injured parties each filed a lawsuit to recover damages for injuries they received while working on separate construction sites.


OVERVIEW: The critical inquiry was whether Public Act 89-2 constituted an unconditional repeal of the Act. Defendant employers argued that the General Assembly's statement regarding the prospective application of the instant repealer, found in the preamble, could not be construed as a valid saving clause. The court agreed that the preamble to Public Act 89-2 could be used to resolve the issue. However, the court did not believe that it was justified in resorting to the preamble on the basis of an ambiguity in the present statute. There was nothing ambiguous about the language. Public Act 89-2 did not expressly indicate whether it was to be applied prospectively or retroactively. Therefore, a presumption of retroactive application was created, which could be rebutted by an expressed contrary legislative intent. The court held that the expression of contrary legislative intent necessary to rebut that presumption could be gleaned from the preamble itself. The court would be defeating legislative intent, rather than giving effect to it, if it was to accept the employers' contentions that Public Act 89-2 constituted an unconditional repeal of the Act.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of one circuit court, which held that the language of the preamble evinced a legislative intent not to give the repealing statute retrospective intent. It reversed the judgment of the other circuit court, which held that the preamble was not part of the repealing act itself. The court held that all such causes of action, which accrued prior to the effective date of the repeal of the Act, were preserved.

People v. Smith


Docket No. 77078


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


177 Ill. 2d 53; 685 N.E.2d 880; 1997 Ill. LEXIS 394; 226 Ill. Dec. 425



June 19, 1997, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied September 29, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court of Cook County, the Hon. John W. Crilly, Judge, presiding.



DISPOSITION: Convictions affirmed; death sentence vacated; cause remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder and conspiracy to commit murder. The Circuit Court of Cook County (Illinois) denied defendant's post-trial motion, which sought a new trial to introduce exculpatory testimony of nine new witnesses. The trial judge sentenced defendant to death because she procured another to commit murder for money and mitigating factors were insufficient to preclude the death penalty. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged her convictions and death sentence on various grounds, including trial error, ineffective assistance, sentencing errors, and the constitutionality of the Illinois death penalty statute. Defendant had hired another to murder her lover's wife. The court affirmed the convictions, but vacated the death sentence. In so doing, the court held that (1) the State was able to provide independent corroboration of many important aspects of the accomplice's testimony, and given the totality of the evidence, and after review of it in the light most favorable to the prosecution, any rational trier of fact could have found defendant guilty beyond a reasonable doubt; (2) defendant's newly discovered evidence, which offered to impeach the accomplice testimony, was an insufficient basis for granting a new trial; and (3) while defendant's jealousy and rage were no excuse for the murder, she had no criminal history except for this one explosive episode and the retributive and deterrent functions of the death penalty would not have been served by putting her alone to death for this offense. The court did not reach the issue of the constitutionality of the death penalty statute.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were affirmed, but her death sentence was vacated. The case was remanded with directions to impose a sentence other than death.

People v. Madej


Docket No. 77167


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


177 Ill. 2d 116; 685 N.E.2d 908; 1997 Ill. LEXIS 396; 226 Ill. Dec. 453



June 19, 1997, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected August 13, 1997. Rehearing Denied September 29, 1997. Certiorari Denied April 27, 1998, Reported at: 1998 U.S. LEXIS 2892.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court of Cook County, the Hon. Vincent Bentivenga, Judge, presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order from the Circuit Court of Cook County (Illinois), which dismissed appellant's amended petition for relief pursuant to the Post-Conviction Hearing Act, Ill. Rev. Stat., ch. 38, para. 122-1 et seq. (1985), without an evidentiary hearing. Appellant attacked his conviction for murder and his death sentence by contending that his right to effective assistance at trial and during sentencing was violated.


OVERVIEW: The circuit court convicted appellant of murder, felony murder, and rape and sentenced him to death. The circuit court dismissed appellant's amended petition for relief pursuant to the Post-Conviction Hearing Act, Ill. Rev. Stat., ch. 38, para. 122-1 et seq. (1985). The court affirmed, holding that even though appellant was denied effective assistance of counsel during the aggravation/mitigation phase of trial, appellant failed to demonstrate that he suffered any prejudice as a result of his attorney's failure to conduct an adequate investigation and present a full mitigation defense because there was no reasonable probability that the outcome of his sentencing hearing would have been different. Appellant's attorney's decision requiring appellant to testify at trial failed to satisfy the prejudice component of the Strickland analysis because there was overwhelming evidence of appellant's guilt. Appellant's attorney's alleged failure to present additional evidence in support of appellant's claim of diminished mental capacity was trial strategy and was not ineffective assistance because the omitted evidence was cumulative and would not have changed the trial's outcome.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order from the circuit court that dismissed appellant's request for post-conviction relief from his conviction and death sentence. Appellant was not prejudiced by ineffective assistance of counsel during sentencing because the outcome of his sentencing hearing would not have been different if his attorney had investigated and prepared more adequately for the aggravation/mitigation phase of the trial.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Circuit Court of Cook County (Illinois) accepted defendant's plea of guilty to four counts of murder, conducted a capital sentencing hearing, and imposed the death penalty. Defendant's post-sentencing motions for a new sentence based on the state's improper conduct at the sentencing hearing and to withdraw her guilty plea were denied. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to four counts of murder. At the capital sentencing hearing, the jury found that defendant was eligible for the death penalty, and the death penalty was imposed. Defendant's post-sentencing motions were denied. On appeal, defendant claimed that the state's closing argument during the sentencing hearing was improper and prejudicial, that the trial court erred in prohibiting the display of her children during the mitigation, and that the admission of an inmate's testimony was error. OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was affirmed. The imposition of the death penalty was vacated, and the case was remanded for rehearing with instructions that defendant was entitled to 14 peremptory challenges.

People v. B.C. (in Re B.C.)


Docket No. 80715


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


176 Ill. 2d 536; 680 N.E.2d 1355; 1997 Ill. LEXIS 54; 223 Ill. Dec. 919



May 22, 1997, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Appellate Court for the Fifth District; heard in that court on appeal from the Circuit Court of Madison County, the Hon. Lola Maddox, Judge, presiding.



DISPOSITION: Judgments reversed; cause remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Appellate Court for the Fifth District (Illinois) affirmed the decision of the circuit court dismissing the state's petitions charging defendants as delinquent minors for committing the offense of disorderly conduct in violation of 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. §§ 5/12-7.1 and 5/26-1 (1994). The state appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendants displayed a hand drawing depicting acts of violence against African-Americans by hooded Ku Klux Klansmen and gang symbols declaring "Supreme White Power" to an individual who was not an African-American, but who in the presence of African-Americans. Defendants alleged that, because the alleged victim was not an African-American and was not perceived to be an African-American, they could not be convicted of a hate crime pursuant to 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/12-7.1(a) (1994). The state alleged it was the action and not the nature of the victim that was pertinent to an offense under the statute. The court concluded the legislature intended that it was the improper bias that motivated a hate crime and that substantiated the elements of the offense and not the status of a particular victim. There was no indication that the legislature intended only to redress wrongs caused by a biased selection of victims. The offense of disorderly conduct included not only violent acts, but words and acts likely to produce violence in others. Defendants displayed such conduct by the nature of the items they displayed to the victim.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decisions of the appellate court and the circuit court that dismissed the state's petitions against defendants as delinquent minors for committing the offense of disorderly conduct. The court remanded to the circuit court for further proceedings.

 

91 of 1262 DOCUMENTS


Johnson v. Edgar


Docket Nos. 81019, 81249 cons.


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


176 Ill. 2d 499; 680 N.E.2d 1372; 1997 Ill. LEXIS 52; 224 Ill. Dec. 1



May 22, 1997, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] No. 81019 - Appeal from the Circuit Court of Cook County, the Hon. Aaron Jaffe, Judge, presiding.

No. 81249 - Appeal from the Circuit Court of Madison County, the Hon. Lewis E. mallott, Judge, presiding.



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed, as modified.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated appeals from orders of the Circuit Courts of Cook County and of Madison County (Illinois), defendants (the State and various government officials) challenged under Ill. Sup. Ct. R. 302(a) the circuit court rulings in each case. The circuit court held that Public Act 89-428 was unconstitutional and permanently enjoined defendants from enforcing any provision of that Act.


OVERVIEW: In two different actions, the circuit courts held that Public Act 89-428 was unconstitutional and permanently enjoined defendants from enforcing any provision of it. The circuit courts had held that Public Act 89-428 was unconstitutional in its entirety because it encompassed more than one subject in violation of Ill. Const. art. IV, § 8(d), otherwise known as the single subject rule. On appeal, the court held that Public Act 89-428 was enacted in violation of the single subject rule because it encompassed a multitude of provisions covering many legislated areas. However, the court also agreed that application of Public Act 89-457 required that the injunctions be lifted to the extent that those injunctions prohibited the use, pursuant to Public Act 89-457, of environmental impact fees already collected under Public Act 89-428, as Public Act 89-457 met the test for curative legislation and because no party in the case had challenged the constitutionality of applying it as curative legislation. As such, the circuit courts' injunctions needed to be lifted to the extent they proscribed the use of fees that had already been collected under Public Act 89-428.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the circuit courts declaring the Act unconstitutional and enjoining its enforcement, except that the court vacated the portion of the circuit courts' injunctions that prohibited the use of environmental impact fees already collected under another Act, which was considered curative legislation.

 

92 of 1262 DOCUMENTS


In re Marriage of Lappe


Docket No. 81605


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


176 Ill. 2d 414; 680 N.E.2d 380; 1997 Ill. LEXIS 51; 223 Ill. Dec. 647



May 1, 1997, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court of Madison County, the Hon. Wendell Durr, Judge, presiding.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Illinois Department of Public Aid challenged an order by the Circuit Court of Madison County (Illinois), which denied its motion to intervene on behalf of the ex-husband to obtain a child support award from appellee ex-wife.


OVERVIEW: In denying the department's motion to intervene, the circuit court ruled that §§ 10-1 and 10-10 of the Public Aid Code, codified at 305 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/10-1 and 10-10 (1994), violated Ill. Const. art. VIII, § 1 (1970) as applied to the ex-husband because he was of sufficient financial means to hire an attorney to pursue child support. The department argued that by intervening on behalf of the ex-husband it was not using public funds for a private purpose. The court held that it had jurisdiction under Ill. Sup. Ct. R. 302(a)(1) because the circuit court declared portions of 305 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/10-1 and 10-10 unconstitutional on their face. The court determined that §§ 10-1 and 10-10 clearly authorized the department to intervene and to petition to establish child support on behalf of the ex-husband regardless of his financial need. The court held that the ex-wife, as the party challenging the statute's constitutionality, failed to meet her burden of establishing that no public purpose was served by the provision of child support enforcement services. The department's services were at aimed at helping the child, not the parent, and therefore they served a public purpose.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order and remanded the case to the circuit court for further proceedings consistent with its opinion.

 

93 of 1262 DOCUMENTS


People v. Burgess


Docket No. 79162


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


176 Ill. 2d 289; 680 N.E.2d 357; 1997 Ill. LEXIS 47; 223 Ill. Dec. 624



April 24, 1997, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected May 28, 1997. Certiorari Denied December 1, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 7145. Petition for Rehearing Denied June 2, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court of Henry County, the Hon. Jay M. Hanson, Judge, presiding.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review from a judgment of the Circuit Court of Henry County (Illinois), which convicted him of first degree murder and aggravated battery of a child, and imposed a term of imprisonment as well as a death sentence. Defendant contended that he was entitled to a new hearing because he had received psychotropic drugs at the time of his trial and sentencing, but had no hearing as to his fitness.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder and aggravated battery of a child, and was sentenced to imprisonment as well as to death. At the time of trial and sentencing, defendant had been receiving psychotropic drugs for sleep. Defendant appealed, contending that he was entitled to a new trial because he had not been given a fitness hearing, as required by 725 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/104-21(a). The court remanded for a supplementary hearing, at which time the trial court concluded that the drugs had no effect on defendant's mental functioning. The court then held that retrospective hearings were acceptable on a case by case basis, and that the extent of the hearing on remand was appropriate. The court affirmed the conviction, holding that evidence of prior acts of abuse was properly admitted to show intent and absence of accident. The court found no merit in defendant's claim that counsel was ineffective for failing to object to one of two aggravating factors, where the outcome would not have changed. Lastly, the court held that 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/9-1 (1994), the statute imposing the death sentence, was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for first degree murder and aggravated battery of a child, and his sentence of death, holding that a retrospective hearing as to defendant's fitness was proper and provided sufficient evidence that he was fit for trial. Further, that the statute imposing death was constitutional, and that there was no merit to allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel or evidentiary errors.

Aleckson v. Round Lake Park


Docket No. 79668


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


176 Ill. 2d 82; 679 N.E.2d 1224; 1997 Ill. LEXIS 42; 223 Ill. Dec. 451



April 17, 1997, Opinion Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Appellate Court for the Second District; heard in that court on appeal from the Circuit Court of Lake County, the Hon. Jack Hoogasian, Judge, presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, four members of a police department, filed an action for declaratory and injunctive relief in the Circuit Court of Lake County, naming as defendants, a village and its officials, alleging that defendants violated various sections of the Illinois Municipal Code during a promotional examination. Defendants appealed because an intervening appellate court decision modifying time limitations was not applied retroactively.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, four members of a police department, filed suit against defendants, a village and its officials, alleging that various provisions of the Illinois Municipal Code, 65 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/10-2.1-1 et seq., were violated by the officials in administering a promotional examination. Relying upon previous holdings of the appellate court, the suit seeking injunctive and declarative relief was filed within one year of the occurrence, but after the suit was filed, in an unrelated case, the same appellate court reversed its prior rulings and applied the 35 day administrative review limitation to the situation. In holding that it was proper for the appellate court to apply the decision prospectively only, the court ruled that as the decision broke with past precedent, to give it retroactive effect would have caused injustice and hardship. It would have been substantially inequitable to hold that plaintiffs slept on their rights at a time when they could not have known the time limitations the law imposed upon them.


OUTCOME: The court upheld the appellate court's prospective application of its ruling applying an administrative review deadline because retroactive application to plaintiffs, four members of a police department, relying on prior holdings of the same court would have been inequitable.

People v. Pulliam


Docket No. 78406


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


176 Ill. 2d 261; 680 N.E.2d 343; 1997 Ill. LEXIS 44; 223 Ill. Dec. 610



April 17, 1997, Opinion Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied October 14, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 6155. Petition for Rehearing Denied June 2, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court of Cook County, the Hon. Dennis J. Porter and the Hon. John W. Crilly, Judges, presiding.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury in the Circuit Court of Cook County (Illinois) found a defendant guilty of first degree murder, 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/9-1(a)(1) (1992), aggravated criminal sexual assault, 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/12-14(a)(2) (1992), and aggravated kidnapping, 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/9-1(b)(6) (1992). Defendant was sentenced to death and consecutive prison terms. Defendant appealed her convictions and sentences.


OVERVIEW: Defendant appealed her convictions of first degree murder, aggravated criminal sexual assault, and aggravated kidnapping and her sentences alleging that the trial court erred in several ways including admitting hearsay, allowing testimony concerning a court-ordered fitness examination at trial and sentencing, allowing various items of evidence in aggravation, and imposing an excessive sentence. The court held (1) a police officer's testimony repeating comments made by bystanders as he was pursuing the defendant was not hearsay because it was not offer to prove the truth of the matter asserted but was offered to show investigative steps leading to the arrest, (2) defendant waived any error in admission of the fitness exam at trial and at sentencing by failing to object when the evidence was offered, (3) evidence of the defendant's participation in other crimes was admissible because it was relevant and reliable, (4) the sentence was not excessive because mitigation evidence of defendant's cognitive abilities and mental health did not preclude imposition of a death sentence when that evidence was outweighed by aggravating evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the defendant's convictions and sentences.

People v. Smith

176 Ill. 2d 217; 680 N.E.2d 291; 1997 Ill. LEXIS 28; 223 Ill. Dec. 558

Writ of certiorari denied Smith v. Illinois, 522 U.S. 920, 139 L. Ed. 2d 241, 118 S. Ct. 311, 1997 U.S. LEXIS 6138 (1997)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at People v. Smith, 195 Ill. 2d 179, 745 N.E.2d 1194, 2000 Ill. LEXIS 1714, 253 Ill. Dec. 660 (2000)
Post-conviction proceeding at, Remanded by People v. Smith, 2004 Ill. App. LEXIS 1142 (Ill. App. Ct., Sept. 24, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court of Cook County, the Hon. Themis N. Karnezis, Judge, presiding.
People v. Smith, 152 Ill. 2d 229, 604 N.E.2d 858, 1992 Ill. LEXIS 143, 178 Ill. Dec. 335 (1992)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Circuit Court of Cook County (Illinois) convicted defendant of first degree murder, aggravated criminal sexual assault, and home invasion and sentenced him to death for the murder conviction and to a term of imprisonment for the other convictions. When the sentence of death was reimposed the death penalty on remand, the court stayed the sentence and reviewed the case under Ill. Const. of 1970, art. VI, § 4(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was sentenced to death for a first degree murder conviction. The trial court reimposed the death sentence on remand. The court reviewed the case under Ill. Const. of 1970, art. VI, § 4(b). Defendant asserted that he was deprived of the right to a fair sentencing hearing. The court held that (1) defendant's waiver of his right to a jury was made knowingly, intelligently, and voluntarily, (2) counsel's mistake did not constitute ineffective assistance of counsel because there was no showing that it prejudiced the defense, (3) defendant waived his right to testify under 720 Ill. Comp. OUTCOME: The sentence of death was affirmed.

People v. Davis


Docket No. 81358


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


177 Ill. 2d 495; 687 N.E.2d 24; 1997 Ill. LEXIS 444; 227 Ill. Dec. 101



March 7, 1997, Decided  
October 17, 1997, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court of Cook County. CASE NUMBER: TR95CR34051. Hon. Dennis J. Porter, TRIAL JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with state weapons charges and filed a motion to dismiss. The Circuit Court of Cook County (Illinois) found that a sentencing disparity under the Firearm Owners Identification Card Act (Act), 430 Ill. Comp. Stat. 65/0.01 et seq., was disproportionate under Ill. Const. art. I, § 11, and that the disparity violated due process protections under Ill. Const. art. I, § 2 (1970); U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The state appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged by information with unlawful use of a weapon by a felon in violation of 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/24-1.1(a), unlawful use of a weapon in violation of 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/24-1(a)(4), and one count of failing to have a firearm owner's card in violation of 430 Ill. Comp. Stat. 65/2(a)(1) of the act. Prior to trial, the defendant filed a motion to dismiss the charge for violation of the Act. The defendant contended that a disparity in the penalties for possessing a firearm without a firearm owner's card, 730 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/5-5-3(c)(2)(N), and for unlawful use of a weapon by a felon, 730 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/5-8-1(a)(6), violated the due process provisions of Ill. Const. art. I, § 2 (1970), and U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The trial court found that a sentencing disparity was unconstitutional. The state appealed. On appeal, the court held that the penalty possessing a firearm without a firearm owner's card, when compared to the penalty for unlawful use of a weapon by a felon, violated the proportionate penalties clause, Ill. Const. art. I, § 11 (1970). It was disproportionate to make the failure to have the card a more serious offense than having the firearm.


OUTCOME: The judgment holding the penalty for violations of a state statute unconstitutional was affirmed, one charge against the defendant was dismissed, and the case was remanded for further proceedings.

People v. Dean


Docket No. 79750


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


175 Ill. 2d 244; 677 N.E.2d 947; 1997 Ill. LEXIS 18; 222 Ill. Dec. 413



February 20, 1997, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Appellate court judgment reversed; circuit court judgment reversed; cause remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of aggravated criminal sexual assault of his five-year-old stepdaughter in violation of 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/12-14(b)(1). He was sentenced to 30 years' imprisonment. The appellate court (Illinois) affirmed defendant's conviction and sentence, and defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that he was deprived at trial of his constitutional right to a face-to-face confrontation with the victim, whom the trial court permitted to testify outside defendant's presence by closed circuit television pursuant to the Child Shield Act, 725 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/106B-1. The court held that the 1994 amendment to Ill. Const. art. I, § 8 and the subsequently reenacted Child Shield Act did not apply retroactively to defendant's case. Consequently, at the time of his trial, defendant was entitled to meet witnesses face to face. Defendant was deprived of that constitutional right when the victim testified by closed circuit television. Moreover, the court concluded that the appellate court erred in applying the constitutional amendment and reenacted Child Shield Act retroactively to cure the error of admitting the victim's testimony via closed circuit television. The court found that the error in admitting the victim's testimony by closed circuit television contributed to the trial court's decision to convict defendant. The error therefore was not harmless beyond a reasonable doubt. On retrial, defendant would be allowed to confront witnesses face to face.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's judgment of conviction and remanded the cause for a new trial.

Marketview Motors, Inc. v. Colonial Ins. Co. of CA.


Docket No. 80665


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


175 Ill. 2d 460; 677 N.E.2d 870; 1997 Ill. LEXIS 16; 222 Ill. Dec. 336



February 6, 1997, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Appellate court judgment reversed; circuit court judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant insurance company appealed from an Illinois appellate court, which reversed a trial court's grant of summary judgment to the insurance company in a declaratory judgment action brought by plaintiff car dealer. The appellate court held that § 143.15 of the Illinois Insurance Code, codified at 215 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/143.15 (1994), required 10 days actual notice before canceling an insurance policy for premium nonpayment.


OVERVIEW: A buyer purchased an automobile from a car dealer, although the car dealer retained a lien against the vehicle. When the buyer later failed to pay his insurance premium, the insurance company mailed a notice to both the buyer and the car dealer indicating that the policy would be cancelled 10 days after the date of the notice. The buyer's automobile was subsequently involved in an accident on either the 11th or 12th day after the date of the notice, and the car dealer demanded payment for the loss. The insurance company denied payment on the basis that the accident occurred more than 10 days after the mailing of the notice. The car dealer filed a declaratory judgment action, but the trial court granted summary judgment to the insurance company. On appeal, the appellate court reversed, ruling that § 5/143.15 required 10 days actual notice before cancellation for nonpayment of premium. On further appeal, the court reversed. Specifically, the court held that the explicit language and the legislative history of § 5/143.15 indicate that the legislature did not intend to impose an actual notice requirement on an insurance company when canceling a policy for nonpayment of premium.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's ruling that 10 days of actual notice was required before the insurance company could cancel an automobile policy for nonpayment of premiums. Instead, the court upheld the trial court's decision that the insurance company had been justified in cancelling a policy 10 days after the mailing of the notice to the policy holder and the car dealer.

People v. Truitt


Docket No. 80540


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


175 Ill. 2d 148; 676 N.E.2d 665; 1997 Ill. LEXIS 4; 221 Ill. Dec. 862



January 30, 1997, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Appeal dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of the judgment of the Rock Island County Court (Illinois), which held that Ill. Code Civ. P., 725 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/115-15, was unconstitutional and that the state would be required to present testimony from the person who analyzed the substance in question and prepared the report, instead of just presenting the report.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with the unlawful delivery of a controlled substance. Prior to trial, the state hoped to avail itself of § 115-15, which allowed the state to present the laboratory report as prima facie evidence of the contents of a substance, instead of presenting the person who analyzed the substance. Defense counsel objected to the procedure. The trial court entered an order declaring that § 115-15 was unconstitutional and could not be invoked by the state to avoid presenting testimony. Upon review, the court dismissed the state's appeal. The court held that it did not have jurisdiction over the appeal. The state asserted that the court had jurisdiction pursuant to 134 Ill. 2d R. 604, which allowed the state to appeal rulings that dealt with suppression of evidence. The court found that Rule 604 was inapplicable to the case at bar because the trial court's order did not have the effect of suppressing evidence. The order would not prevent any facts or opinions from being presented to the jury. Instead of being able to rely on a piece of paper, the state would have to present live testimony. In fact, the jury would end up receiving additional evidence through live testimony.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the state's appeal of the trial court's order that required the state to present live testimony regarding the analysis of the substance in question.

People v. P.S. (In re P.S.)

January 30, 1997, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of an appellate decision vacating defendant's drug-related conviction. The United States Supreme Court vacated that judgment and remanded.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested after he was observed returning to his car after purchasing drugs, and the state filed an in rem forfeiture action against his car while the criminal charge was pending. After entry of the forfeiture judgment, defendant moved to dismiss the drug possession charge on grounds that the forfeiture was punishment under the Double Jeopardy Clauses of the United States and Illinois Constitutions. The court held that because the forfeiture action was civil in nature, subsequent criminal prosecution for the same underlying conduct did not implicate double jeopardy concerns.


OUTCOME: The court reversed its prior judgment and remanded the matter for further proceedings in defendant's prosecution for drug possession charges.

People v. McNeal


Docket No. 78736


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


175 Ill. 2d 335; 677 N.E.2d 841; 1997 Ill. LEXIS 6; 222 Ill. Dec. 307

Post-conviction relief dismissed at People v. McNeal, 194 Ill. 2d 135, 742 N.E.2d 269, 2000 Ill. LEXIS 1390, 252 Ill. Dec. 19 (2000)

OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with two murders after a gun was recovered in a warrantless search of his garbage can, proved to be the murder weapon. People v. Lewis


Docket No. 80318


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


175 Ill. 2d 412; 677 N.E.2d 830; 1996 Ill. LEXIS 128; 222 Ill. Dec. 296



December 19, 1996, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with robbery, armed violence predicated on robbery committed with a category I weapon, and armed robbery. The Circuit Court of Cook County (Illinois) concluded that the penalties were disproportionate and dismissed the armed violence count. The state appealed.


OVERVIEW: The issue on appeal was whether the penalties for armed robbery, 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/18-2 (1994), and armed violence predicated on robbery committed with a category I weapon, 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/33A-I (1994), were proportionate under Ill. Const. art. I, § 11. The court affirmed the trial court's order. It held the penalty for armed violence predicated on robbery committed with a category I weapon violated the proportionate penalties clause of art. I, § 11. The armed violence and armed robbery offenses were substantively identical, and they contained illogically disparate penalties. Because the mandatory minimum penalty for armed violence predicated on robbery committed with a category I weapon violated the constitutional guarantee of proportionate sentencing, the state had no authority to charge that offense.


OUTCOME: The order dismissing the armed violence predicated on robbery committed with a category I weapon charge against defendant was affirmed because that charge and the armed robbery charge were unconstitutionally disproportionate.

 

105 of 1262 DOCUMENTS


People v. Krueger


Docket No. 80486


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


175 Ill. 2d 60; 675 N.E.2d 604; 1996 Ill. LEXIS 130; 221 Ill. Dec. 409



December 19, 1996, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied October 6, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 4603.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The defendant was charged with unlawful possession of a controlled substance, unlawful possession of a controlled substance with intent to deliver, and armed violence. The Circuit Court of Lake County (Illinois) granted the defendant's motion to quash his arrest and suppress the evidence. The circuit court declared 725 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/108-8(b)(2) (1994), a subsection of the no-knock statute, unconstitutional. The state appealed.


OVERVIEW: A search warrant authorized unannounced entry of the defendant's home under 725 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/108-8(b)(2) (1994) based on the fact that the defendant was known to keep a firearm there. The police obtained evidence by entering the defendant's home without knocking and announcing their office. On appeal, the court held that 725 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/108-8(b)(2) (1994) was unconstitutional because classification of prior firearm possession as an exigent circumstance authorizing a no-knock entry violated the reasonableness requirement. No exigent circumstance existed because there was no evidence the police had a reasonable belief that an occupant of the defendant's residence would have used a firearm against the police if they had proceeded with the ordinary announcements. The unannounced entry violated the defendant's rights under the Fourth Amendment of the United States Constitution and Ill. Const. of 1970, art. I, § 6. The evidence seized from the defendant's home was properly excluded because Ill. Const. of 1970, art. I, § 6 prohibited an exception to Illinois' exclusionary rule.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the circuit court's judgment quashing the defendant's arrest, suppressing the evidence obtained from the unannounced entry into the defendant's home, and declaring unconstitutional the subsection of the no-knock statute that classified prior possession of a firearm as an exigent circumstance authorizing a no-knock entry.

 

106 of 1262 DOCUMENTS


Mark Twain Ill. Bank v. Hicks (Estate of Hicks)


Docket Nos. 80604, 80677 cons.


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


174 Ill. 2d 433; 675 N.E.2d 89; 1996 Ill. LEXIS 131; 221 Ill. Dec. 182



December 19, 1996, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Judgment affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant administrator sought review of a judgment of the Circuit Court of St. Clair County (Illinois), which found 755 Ill. Comp. Stat. § 5/2-2(d) unconstitutional and, therefore, ordered distribution of administrator's decedent's estate on the basis of 755 Ill. Comp. Stat. § 5/2-1 because decedent was an intestate minor.


OVERVIEW: Decedent was an intestate illegitimate minor. Pursuant to 755 Ill. Comp. Stat. § 5/2-2(d), decedent's mother and half-sisters were identified as decedent's only heirs. Appellee father filed a motion to set aside the heirship order and the order appointing the administrator of decedent's estate. Father alleged he had paid child support and had established paternity in court prior to decedent's death. The circuit court granted father's motion, finding that § 2-2(d) violated Ill. Const. art. I, § 18, by unlawfully discriminating on the basis of gender. On appeal, the court held that while the state had a legitimate interest in distributing an intestate illegitimate minor's estate to a parent who acknowledged and supportted the child, § 2-2(d) was not narrowly tailored to employ the least restrictive means for accomplishing the purpose. As § 2-2(d) did not withstand strict scrutiny, § 2-1(d) governed distribution of the estate. As administrator was only properly bringing the appeal on behalf of the half-sisters, costs were improperly charged to decedent's estate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the circuit court's order. The court reversed the assessment of costs to the estate because the administrator could only bring the appeal on behalf of aggrieved parties, not the estate.

People v. Kidd


Docket No. 76490


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


175 Ill. 2d 1; 675 N.E.2d 910; 1996 Ill. LEXIS 132; 221 Ill. Dec. 486



December 19, 1996, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected February 4, 1997. Certiorari Denied June 9, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 3631.
Rehearing denied by, 02/03/1997
Writ of certiorari denied Kidd v. Illinois, 520 U.S. 1269, 117 S. Ct. 2443, 138 L. Ed. 2d 203, 1997 U.S. LEXIS 3631 (1997)
Post-conviction relief dismissed at People v. Kidd, 2016 IL App (1st) 122605-U, 2016 Ill. App. Unpub. LEXIS 606 (2016)


PRIOR HISTORY: People v. Kidd, 129 Ill. 2d 432, 544 N.E.2d 704, 1989 Ill. LEXIS 103, 136 Ill. Dec. 18 (1989)



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed in part and reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted by a jury of murder, armed robbery, aggravated arson, and concealment of a homicidal death in the Circuit Court of Cook County (Illinois). The same jury found that defendant was eligible for the death penalty. Defendant was then sentenced to death for the murder convictions and received sentences of imprisonment for the other convictions and defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the trial court did not err in failing to grant defendant's motion to quash defendant's arrest and to suppress evidence that stemmed from the arrest. The court determined that there was sufficient probable cause for the arrest and the totality of the circumstances fully supported the police officers' reliance on a tip provided. The court further held that there was no error in the denial of defendant's motion to suppress statements he made. The court held that the evidence produced at trial was properly admitted and that any error in the introduction of testimony was harmless. The court also held that identification of defendant was valid because it was based upon an independent origin. The court further held that it was proper to allow the jury to see photographs of the crimes. The court determined that the State's closing arguments, while improper, were not prejudicial and did not require reversal. Defendant's conviction for aggravated arson was reversed because the statute had been previously been declared unconstitutional. Finally, the court held that the sentences were proper and that any error in the sentencing proceeding was either cured or was harmless.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for murder, armed robbery, and concealment of a homicidal death but reversed defendant's conviction for aggravated arson. The court upheld defendant's sentence of death.

Lee v. Nationwide Cassel, L.P.


Docket No. 80465


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS

People v. Haynes


Docket No. 77569


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


174 Ill. 2d 204; 673 N.E.2d 318; 1996 Ill. LEXIS 120; 220 Ill. Dec. 406



October 24, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied May 19, 1997, Reported at 1997 U.S. LEXIS 3146.



DISPOSITION: Convictions affirmed in part and vacated in part; sentences affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the trial court (Illinois), which convicted defendant of murder and burglary and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: Subsequent to his conviction and imposition of his sentence, defendant argued that his waiver of a jury proceeding to determine his competency to stand trial was not valid. On review, however, the court held that defendant was a party that could waive his right to a jury in a competency proceeding. Thus, the court concluded that the waiver was valid. The court then held that the trial court did not err in determining that defendant was fit to stand trial. Additionally, the court held that defendant's waiver of his right to an attorney was valid. The court then held that the trial court erred in failing to offer a separate sentencing hearing on defendant's eligibility for the death penalty. However, under the circumstances, the court held that the error did not require rehearing on the issue. The court then held that a constitutionally valid statutory aggravating factor supported the imposition of the death penalty, and that the death penalty itself was not unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for intentional murder and burglary, but vacated defendant's convictions for knowing and felony murder. The court then affirmed the death sentence.

People v. Nash

SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS

DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution sought review of the order by the Circuit Court of Cook County (Illinois) dismissing the complaints against the defendants on the basis that the statute under which the defendants were charged violated the First and Fourteenth Amendments of the United States Constitution.


OVERVIEW: The defendants were charged under a statute that had, more than 25 years previously, been declared unconstitutional and against which a permanent injunction, barring the statute's enforcement, had been issued by a federal court. In this case, the court determined that the language in the complaint consisted only of a confusing jumble of allegations insufficient to sustain a prosecution for any offense. Because the complaints failed to charge a crime properly, the court concluded that the complaints were properly dismissed.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the circuit court was affirmed because the complaints filed against the defendants were fatally defective.

 

111 of 1262 DOCUMENTS


Fink v. Ryan


Docket No. 79404


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


174 Ill. 2d 302; 673 N.E.2d 281; 1996 Ill. LEXIS 108; 65 U.S.L.W. 2260; 220 Ill. Dec. 369



October 18, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied June 27, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 4067.



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Secretary of State sought review of the order of the circuit court of Lake County (Illinois) that found 625 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/11-501.6 (1994) of the Illinois Vehicle Code unconstitutional under the Fourth Amendment.


OVERVIEW: Appellee driver was issued a traffic ticket for failure to reduce speed to avoid an accident. Driver consented to a blood test. Driver was notified his driver's license was to be suspended and he was charged with driving under the influence of alcohol. This declaratory judgment action followed. 625 Ill. Comp. Stat. § 5/11-501.6 (1994) provided that if a traffic accident occurred in which death or personal injury resulted and a driver involved in the accident had been issued a Uniform Traffic Ticket for a non-equipment offense, the driver could be subject to chemical testing to determine whether the driver was impaired by drugs or alcohol. The court held that under the statute a driver would be subject to chemical testing only in situations in which the intrusiveness of the search has been reduced and a driver's expectation of privacy has been diminished. The court concluded that the statute fell within the "special needs" exception to the Fourth Amendment. Next, the court held that the statute did not violate Ill. Const. art. I, § 6, the right to be protected against unreasonable searches and seizures, for the same reason that it found no violation of the Fourth Amendment.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the circuit court was reversed and the cause was remanded for further proceedings.

 

112 of 1262 DOCUMENTS


McNamee v. State


Docket No. 79592


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


173 Ill. 2d 433; 672 N.E.2d 1159; 1996 Ill. LEXIS 111; 220 Ill. Dec. 147



October 18, 1996, FILED



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the State, the governor, and the director of insurance (collectively State), sought review of a decision by the Circuit Court, Cook County (Illinois) that granted summary judgment in favor of plaintiffs, police pension funds and police officers, in their action seeking declaratory and injunctive relief against the State, finding that 40 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/3-127 (1992) violated protected benefit rights under Ill. Const. art. XIII, § 5.


OVERVIEW: The amendment to the maintenance provision of the pension funds changed the method of computing benefits. Section 5 provided that state or local pension or retirement benefits could not be diminished or impaired. The police pension funds and police officers alleged that the amendment violated their constitutionally protected right to the "benefit" of the more secure fund created by the prior funding method. After the police pension funds and police officers were granted summary judgment, the State contended that the amendment merely changed the computation of funding, not constitutionally protected benefits. After reviewing the legislative intent of § 5, the court held that the clearly expressed intention of the framers was to protect public pension benefits, but not to control funding. The court held that the police pension funds and police officers' constitutionally protected rights to benefits were not violated because they did not contend that the amendment diminished their right to receive pension benefits and did not allege that the new funding method would impair benefits by placing the funds on the verge of default or imminent bankruptcy.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the grant of summary judgment in favor of the police pension funds and police officers and remanded for further proceedings.

On a second appeal, the court affirmed the order of the appellate court. The court stated that the employer made an overly cautious decision after an insufficiently thorough investigation which resulted in unfair treatment for the employee. Further, the employer's defense of complying with health regulations failed because AIDS was listed as an contagious disease but not HIV.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court that found the employer had violated the Illinois Human Rights Act.

Committee for Educ. Rights v. Edgar


Docket No. 78198


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


174 Ill. 2d 1; 672 N.E.2d 1178; 1996 Ill. LEXIS 113; 220 Ill. Dec. 166



October 18, 1996, FILED



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff educational reform committee, school boards, and parents and students (those interested in education reform) filed an action against defendant governor, board of education, and state superintendent. The action sought a declaratory judgment that the statutory scheme governing the funding of public schools violated the Illinois Constitution. The trial court dismissed the complaint and the appellate court (Illinois) affirmed.


OVERVIEW: The controversy hinged on the relationship between funding that was derived from local property taxes and funds supplied by the state. Those interested in education reform alleged that the statutory school finance scheme and the failure of the state to provide sufficient funding for preschool educational programs violated the state's equal protection clause, Ill. Const. art. I, § 2, prohibition against special legislation, Ill. Const. art. IV, §13, and education article, Ill. Const. art. X, §1. The trial court dismissed the complaint for failure to state a cause of action. Those interested in education reform appealed the dismissal of the claims under the education article and the equal protection clause only. The court held that disparities in educational funding that resulted from differences in local property wealth did not offend the efficiency requirement found in Ill. Const. art. X, § 1. The court also held that the question of whether the educational institutions and services were high quality was outside the sphere of the judicial function. The court determined that the process of reform had to have been undertaken in a legislative forum rather than in the judicial system.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the complaint.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed the decision of the circuit court of Bureau County (Illinois), which dismissed the charge against defendant for unlawful interference with child visitation rights per a court order. OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with violating the unlawful interference statute, 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/10-5.5. OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part, finding that the unlawful visitation interference statute was not unconstitutional, but the portion restricting the court's contempt powers was violative of separation of powers. The cause was remanded.

 

116 of 1262 DOCUMENTS


Scholtens v. Schneider


Docket No. 79686


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


173 Ill. 2d 375; 671 N.E.2d 657; 1996 Ill. LEXIS 88; 65 U.S.L.W. 2219; 20 Employee Benefits Cas. (BNA) 2379; 219 Ill. Dec. 490



September 19, 1996, FILED



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant trustees sought reimbursement pursuant to a subrogation agreement from appellee beneficiary for funds paid to the beneficiary from an employee benefit plan for injuries received in a car accident after the beneficiary received a settlement in his action against the tortfeasors. Reimbursement was granted but the trial court applied the common fund doctrine to reduce the award and the appellate court (Illinois) affirmed.


OVERVIEW: The employee plan from which the beneficiary received money was governed by the Employee Retirement Income Security Act of 1974 (ERISA), 29 U.S.C.S. § 1001 et seq. The trial court applied the common fund doctrine to reduce the reimbursement amount to account for the attorney fees and costs incurred in the action against the tortfeasors. The trustees claimed that ERISA § 514 codified at 29 U.S.C.S. § 1144, which specified that ERISA preempted state laws related to employee benefit plans, barred application of the common fund doctrine. The court found that § 514 did not bar application of the common fund doctrine in this case because (1) ERISA did not preempt state rules of general application that did not single out or expressly refer to ERISA plans; (2) where the obligations sought to be enforced arose independently of the plan and the relationship or agreements formulated among the principal ERISA entities were not altered, application of the common fund doctrine did not dictate or restrict the manner in which the plan was structured or administered; and (3) the additional costs imposed on the plans by application of the doctrine did not disrupt their uniform administration.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the appellate court, which upheld the trial court's use of the common fund doctrine to reduce reimbursement to trustees of money paid out to beneficiary under an ERISA employee benefit plan.

People v. Shatner

SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant inmate appealed the order from the trial court (Illinois), which entered a jury verdict of guilty of first-degree murder, armed robbery, and arson and which imposed the death penalty.


OVERVIEW: The court found that defendant could not show that there was a reasonable likelihood that, but for counsel's inaction, the result of the eligibility proceeding would have been different. The court found that a valid jury waiver occurred insofar as the trial court explained to defendant that he was waiving the right to have the jury consider the capital sentencing issues and that the sentencing decision would, therefore, be made by the court alone. The court found that the introduction of defendant's gang affiliation evidence was harmless beyond a reasonable doubt and that the religious activities evidence introduced at the sentencing hearing constituted nothing more than irrelevant character evidence. The court found that the sentencing judge was free to conclude that defendant's drug history simply had no mitigating value but was, in fact, aggravating. The court concluded that the trial court did not err in imposing the death penalty under these circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions and the death penalty because it rejected defendant's arguments regarding ineffective assistance of counsel and other claims of error.

People v. Miller


Docket No. 78011


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


173 Ill. 2d 167; 670 N.E.2d 721; 1996 Ill. LEXIS 83; 219 Ill. Dec. 43



August 2, 1996, FILED



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his conviction and sentence of death from the Circuit Court of Peoria County (Illinois) for first degree murder on the grounds that the lower court erred in failing to suppress statements defendant made to police and erred in admitting deoxyribonucleic acid (DNA) evidence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was sentenced to death following his conviction for six counts of first degree murder and he appealed, claiming that the lower court erred in denying defendant's motion to suppress various statements he made to police and that the lower court erred in qualifying an individual to testify about the general acceptance and reliability of deoxyribonucleic acid (DNA) evidence. The court affirmed defendant's conviction and corresponding sentence. The court held that the lower court's decision to deny the motion to suppress was not manifestly erroneous as nothing in the record indicated that defendant's statements were procured through coercion and Miranda warnings given to defendant the day before he made his statements were not so stale and remote that defendant was unaware of his rights. The court also held that the lower court did not abuse its discretion in concluding that a particular individual was an expert on issues involving DNA as the record revealed that through education, training, research, and experience, the individual possessed knowledge which was not common to the average citizen.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction for first degree murder and his resulting death sentence was affirmed on the grounds that evidence suggested that defendant's statements were made in the absence of coercion as defendant was deemed aware of his constitutional rights when he made the statements, and that an individual had the requisite level of knowledge to qualify as an expert on deoxyribonucleic acid (DNA) evidence.

120 of 1262 DOCUMENTS


Trent v. Winningham


Docket Nos. 78726, 78730 cons.


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


172 Ill. 2d 420; 667 N.E.2d 1317; 1996 Ill. LEXIS 80; 217 Ill. Dec. 741



June 20, 1996, FILED



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Cause remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother filed a paternity suit against appellee father in a circuit court (Illinois) seeking retroactive child support under § 14(b) of the Parentage Act, codified at 750 Ill. Comp. Stat. 45/14(b) (1992). The trial court denied retroactive child support, and the mother appealed.


OVERVIEW: The trial court denied the mother retroactive support, claiming that she was not entitled to it, that her claim was time-barred, and that § 14(b) was unconstitutional because it caused unwed fathers to be treated differently from divorced fathers who, under the Illinois Marriage and Dissolution of Marriage Act, did not have to pay similar support. The court held that it was unnecessary to find § 14(b) unconstitutional to dispose of the mother's retroactive support claim against the father. In this particular case, the lynchpin of the court's jurisdiction was the holding that § 14(b) was unconstitutional. Whenever a judgment of the circuit court was grounded upon finding legislation unconstitutional, the mandate of Ill. Sup. Ct. R. 302(a) forced the parties to bypass the normal appellate process. However, the court declined to exercise jurisdiction over the alternative reasons for denying the retroactive support. The court explained that maintaining jurisdiction over the remainder of the circuit court's order would force the court, otherwise a court of permissive review, to contend with issues it might have elected not to address in deference to the appellate court.


OUTCOME: The court ordered that the trial court's judgment which denied an award of retroactive child support to the mother be vacated, modified to exclude the declaration that the section of the Parentage Act was unconstitutional, and immediately reentered.

People v. Williams


Docket No. 76907


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


173 Ill. 2d 48; 670 N.E.2d 638; 1996 Ill. LEXIS 70; 218 Ill. Dec. 916



May 31, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied March 17, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 1747.



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision of the Circuit Court of Cook County (Illinois), which convicted him of first degree murder under Ill. Rev. Stat. ch. 38, para. 9(1)(a)(1) (1991), attempted murder under para. 8-4, and aggravated battery with a firearm under para. 12-4.2(a).

The court held that the trial court's refusal to excuse a venire member for cause was not error because his entire voir dire showed that he could be impartial. The court held that the defendant knowingly, intelligently, and voluntarily waived a jury at sentencing. The court held that the aggravating factors under Ill. Rev. Stat. ch. 38, para. 9-1(b)(11) (1991) were not unconstitutionally vague.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions because the trial court's refusal to excuse a potential juror for cause was not error, the state did not violate Batson, the police could ask about weapons prior to Miranda warnings under the public safety exception, defendant's jury waiver for the sentencing phase of his trial was valid, and the Illinois death penalty statutory aggravating factors were not unconstitutionally vague.

Garza v. Navistar Int'l Transp. Corp.


Docket No. 79293


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


172 Ill. 2d 373; 666 N.E.2d 1198; 1996 Ill. LEXIS 63; 217 Ill. Dec. 260; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P14,642



May 23, 1996, FILED



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Appellate court judgment reversed; circuit court judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant manufacturer appealed the order of an appellate court (Illinois), which reversed the dismissal of the strict liability claim against the manufacturer in plaintiff injured party's action to recover damages for personal injuries that he received in a construction accident.


OVERVIEW: After the injured party was seriously injured by equipment while working as a laborer, he filed an action against the manufacturer and the distributor-lessor. The manufacturer and the distributor-lessor filed motions to dismiss the strict liability claims against them, which were granted by the trial court because they were barred by the repose statute, Ill. Rev. Stat. ch. 110, para. 13-213 (1989). On appeal, the appellate court affirmed the dismissal of the strict liability claim against the distributor, but reversed the dismissal of the claim against the manufacturer because Ill. Rev. Stat. ch. 110, para. 13-213(b) mandated that the 12-year repose provision applied exclusively to manufacturers, while the 10-year repose provision applied to lessors and distributors. On a second appeal, the court reversed the order of the appellate court, holding that there was no language in § 13-213(b) which mandated that the 12-year repose provision applied to manufacturers and that the 10-year provision applied to retailers and lessors. The court stated that the 10-year repose provision applied to both manufacturers and distributors.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and affirmed the judgment of the circuit court.

 

124 of 1262 DOCUMENTS


People v. Redd


Docket No. 76086


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


173 Ill. 2d 1; 670 N.E.2d 583; 1996 Ill. LEXIS 64; 218 Ill. Dec. 861



May 23, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari Denied January 6, 1997, Reported at 1997 U.S. LEXIS 174.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Circuit Court of Cook County (Illinois) of two counts of murder and two counts of rape. He was sentenced to death for the murders and received a prison sentence for the rapes. Defendant appealed pursuant to Ill. Const. art. VI, § 4(b) (1970).


OVERVIEW: Defendant alleged on appeal that his sentence should have been reversed because his waiver of his Sixth Amendment right to counsel was not knowingly and understandingly made. The state argued that defendant waived the issue of the validity of his waiver of counsel because it was not raised in the post-trial motions. He contended that the death penalty violated the Eighth and Fourteenth amendments. Defendant also argued that that he was denied a fair trial because the state was allowed to elicit from the mother of the two victims remarks that defendant believed were prejudicial. The supreme court affirmed the conviction and sentence, concluding that (1) the lower court properly concluded that defendant could understand the significance and consequences of his decision to waive counsel. (2) The defendant was aware of both the process and his right to counsel at sentencing. (3) The state was allowed wide latitude in presenting its closing argument. (4) The evidence against defendant in aggravation was substantial, and defendant offered no evidence in mitigation.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the judgement and ordered the execution of defendant's death sentence.

People v. Gilliam


Docket No. 77771


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


172 Ill. 2d 484; 670 N.E.2d 606; 1996 Ill. LEXIS 65; 218 Ill. Dec. 884



May 23, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied March 3, 1997, Reported at: 1997 U.S. LEXIS 1525.



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with first degree murder, 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/9-1(a)(1), aggravated kidnapping, 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/10-2(a)(3), and robbery, 720 Ill. Comp. Stat. 5/18-1(a). Defendant was convicted in a jury trial before the Circuit Court of Jefferson County (Illinois) and sentenced to death and concurrent 30-year prison terms. Defendant appealed the denial of his motion to suppress evidence, his convictions, and his sentences.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that 1) his confession was involuntary; 2) the trial court made two erroneous rulings regarding his motion to re-open the suppression hearing; 3) venue for the robbery charge properly laid in another county; and 4) the trial court erroneously refused a jury instruction on venue tendered by the defense. The court upheld defendant's convictions in sentences. In so holding, the court found that 1) the trial court did not err in finding that defendant's confession was voluntary where it found the State's witnesses credible and the defendant's account incredible; 2) defendant was collaterally estopped from reopening his suppression hearing none of defendant's proffered evidence was newly discovered since the last hearing; 3) defendant was properly tried in the county where the essence of the crime was continued from the place where the crime began; 4) the trial court properly excused a juror who stated that he would disregard the evidence in order to vote against the death penalty; and 5) it was within the trial court's discretion to refuse to submit defendant's jury instruction where an Illinois Pattern Instruction adequately stated the law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions and sentences, and ordered the clerk to set a date for the defendant's execution.

People v. Washington


Docket No. 76651


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


171 Ill. 2d 475; 665 N.E.2d 1330; 1996 Ill. LEXIS 56; 216 Ill. Dec. 773



April 18, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied June 3, 1996. Dissenting Opinion Dated June 3, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Appellate Court for the First District; heard in that court on appeal from the Circuit Court of Cook County, the Hon. William J. Hibbler, Judge, presiding.

Roland Burris, Attorney General, of Springfield, and Jack O'Malley, State's Attorney, of Chicago (Terence M. Madsen and Arleen C. Anderson, Assistant Attorneys General, of Chicago, and Renee G. Goldfarb, Michael P. Golden and theodore Fotios Burtzos, Assistant State's Attorneys, of counsel), for the People.

Joel T. Pelz, Ellen R. Kordik and Jacob I. Corre, of Jenner & Block, of Chicago, for appellee.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate, convicted of murder, sought post-conviction relief on multiple grounds including a claim of innocence based on newly discovered evidence. The Circuit Court of Cook County (Illinois) granted a new trial and the Appellate Court for the First District affirmed. The state appealed.


OVERVIEW: During the trial court hearing on the inmate's post conviction relief action, the court heard testimony from a witness who revealed evidence that, if true, would have proven the inmate's innocence. On appeal, the court held that the inmate's "free-standing" claim of innocence based on newly discovered evidence could be raised in a petition under the Post-Conviction Hearing Act. Although the relief the inmate sought was not available under U.S. Supreme Court interpretations of the U.S. Constitution, the due process clause of the Illinois Constitution permitted the inmate to raise his claim of innocence in a post-conviction relief action. To deny the additional process when newly discovered evidence indicated innocence was fundamentally unfair and violated procedural due process under the state constitution. To imprison the inmate if he were innocent violated substantive due process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court granting the inmate a new trial.

Desnick v. Department of Professional Regulation


Docket No. 78422


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


171 Ill. 2d 510; 665 N.E.2d 1346; 1996 Ill. LEXIS 59; 216 Ill. Dec. 789; 24 Media L. Rep. 1833



April 18, 1996, FILED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied June 3, 1996. Dissenting Opinion Dated June 3, 1996.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court of Cook County, the Hon. Aaron Jaffe, Judge, presiding.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant professional regulation department sought review of the preliminary injunction granted by the Circuit Court of Cook County (Illinois) to plaintiff ophthalmologist to enjoin the disciplinary proceedings commenced by defendant against plaintiff under the Illinois Medical Practice Act of 1987, Ill. Rev. Stat. ch. 111, para. 4400-22(A)(24), on the ground that the provision was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff ophthalmologist used a telemarketing firm to offer free eye exams and free transportation to the elderly. Defendant professional regulation department commenced disciplinary proceedings against plaintiff under Illinois Medical Practice Act of 1987, Ill. Rev. Stat. ch. 111, para. 4400-22(A)(24). The lower court granted a preliminary injunction to plaintiff against defendant, on the grounds that the regulation was unconstitutional. The court reversed. It held that the section was constitutional as applied to plaintiff's conduct. Under a four-prong test, the court found that plaintiff's targeted telephone solicitations constituted speech protected under U.S. Const. amend. I, that the state had a substantial interest in regulating plaintiff's profession, that the section directly and materially accomplished the prevention of the potential harm of plaintiff's conduct, and that the section was sufficiently narrowly drawn to constitute a reasonable fit between the state's substantial interest and the prohibition against physician's solicitations. Further, the section was not unconstitutionally vague because the term "solicit" had a readily discernible common meaning.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's ruling and the preliminary injunction enjoining disciplinary proceedings by defendant professional regulation department against plaintiff ophthalmologist. The statute relied upon by defendant to prohibit plaintiff's telemarketing scheme was not unconstitutional. The case was remanded.

Brown's Furniture v. Wagner


Docket No. 78195


SUPREME COURT OF ILLINOIS


171 Ill. 2d 410; 665 N.E.2d 795; 1996 Ill. LEXIS 58; 216 Ill. Dec. 537



April 18, 1996, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court of Sangamon County, the Hon. Sue E. Myerscough, Judge, presiding.



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, the Department of Revenue of the State of Illinois and its director (Department), appealed the judgment of the Circuit Court of Sangamon County (Illinois), which entered injunctive relief in favor of plaintiff furniture seller in an action that contested the assessment of a use tax under the Use Tax Act, 35 Ill. Comp. Stat. 105/1 et seq. (1994).


OVERVIEW: After the Department imposed a use tax on the furniture seller, an out-of-state business, on all furniture sold to state residents, the furniture seller obtained an injunction against the assessment of use taxes under the Act. The Department appealed, and the court reversed, holding that the Act applied to the furniture seller because it operated within the state by delivering furniture there. After applying the Complete Auto test, the court concluded that the Act did not violate the dormant Commerce Clause, U.S. Const., art. I, § 8, cl. 3, because it applied to an activity with a substantial nexus with the taxing state, was fairly apportioned, did not discriminate against interstate commerce, and was fairly related to the services provided by the state. Further, the Act did not violate equal protection principles because it was not deliberately based upon an unjustifiable standard and the Department did not exercise its discretion in a discriminatory or arbitrary way. Further, the Department's tax claim was not estopped by any misinformation given by one of its employees to the furniture seller regarding tax liability.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the judgment that awarded injunctive relief to the furniture seller from the assessment of a use tax.
